<div id="review_760252680" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/760252680" />
    <a title="martha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269470-martha"><img alt="martha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1186847950p2/269470.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/760252680?book_show_action=true">Nov 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="martha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="martha" href="/user/show/269470-martha">martha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer760252680" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11290013464596880706">Like a great number of people who have read this book, I am a long-time and ardent Smiths/Morrissey fan. I had been waiting for this book to be published even before I knew there would actually ever be one. With that said, there were a lot of things I liked about it, but also some which I didn't care for.<br/><br/>I once read an interview with Morrissey where he said that everything people needed to know about him was already in his songs, after reading "Autobiography" I truly believe it now.They are defi</span>
  <span id="freeText11290013464596880706" style="display:none">Like a great number of people who have read this book, I am a long-time and ardent Smiths/Morrissey fan. I had been waiting for this book to be published even before I knew there would actually ever be one. With that said, there were a lot of things I liked about it, but also some which I didn't care for.<br /><br />I once read an interview with Morrissey where he said that everything people needed to know about him was already in his songs, after reading "Autobiography" I truly believe it now.They are definitely autobiographical in nature, all of them.I often found myself reading and realizing that particular moments he wrote about in the book, were possibly the inspiration for certain songs. The book opens with him talking about the Manchester of his childhood and the first 160 pages or so are simply wonderful.I often felt like I was getting a peek into Morrissey's private childhood diaries. <br /><br />The detail in which he describes Manchester,his family,childhood and adolescence, made me feel as though I was reading a novel because the manner in which he describes everything truly pulls you in, just like any great novel does. I enjoyed reading about his upbringing because it gave such a great insight into why he is the person that he is today. So many of his obsessions and passions stem from his childhood, such as his fascination with James Dean (his uncle, whom he was close to, was a fan of his and like Dean, he too died young and when Morrissey was still a child). Though a lot of people have criticized his long sentences and few paragraph breaks, those were the things I enjoyed the most. It was complete stream of consciousness writing and it often reminded me of James Joyce's writing style. As I read it, I truly felt the authenticity in his words. It’s written in his voice.<br /><br />The manner in which he writes about the angst he felt at feeling trapped in a life he knew wasn't meant for him, truly resonated with the adolescent in me from long ago. Yet, the thing that stood out the most and perhaps the one thing many fail to see in his music as well, is his humor. He is very funny and sometimes, as he describes something in the book, either serious or sad, he manages to poke fun at himself and a particular situation. I found myself laughing out loud many times as I read. I also enjoyed learning about his deep loyalty towards those whom he loves and cares about, and it’s obvious that when he loves people he is very loyal and kind. It made me understand why when he feels betrayed or hurt he goes after people with a vengeance!<br /> <br />While he definitely used this book to settle some old scores, I didn’t enjoy reading so much of that. However, I will say that I appreciated his honesty in conveying exactly what he feels and thinks about people, men and women alike, whom he deems worthy of his animosity. He doesn't hold back and his words are daggers. When he writes about those whom he loves, his words are equally as intense but incredibly tender and loving. I would’ve simply have preferred less of the angry words. I also didn't always like the detail in which he wrote about certain films or television programs. I felt those things dragged the book down, and it would've been preferable for him to write in that much detail about experiences or feelings that inspired some of his songs. It would’ve also had been great to learn more about him and Johnny Marr as a songwriting team. He has mostly praise for Johnny when he mentions him, which is why the letter Johnny wrote to him after The Smiths split, was one of the saddest things for me to read in the book.<br /> <br />Towards the end, the book feels rushed and as though it was a different book from the first half. It seemed like there hadn’t been as much thought put into it and the prose wasn’t as eloquent as it had been in the first part of it, that was disappointing. I wish his life as an adult had been written with as much detail as his life as a child and teen, but perhaps the reason he chose not to was because “Whatever is sung is the case” (this is written in the acknowledgements page). <br /><br />Although, it’s fair to say that he didn’t shy away from revealing the good and the bad as well as his shortcomings and the insecurities he feels. I wonder how many of us, if given the opportunity to publish our autobiography, would reveal a greater amount of the good versus the not so good. "Autobiography" is definitely worth reading but if you don’t have the time nor the inclination to read nearly 500 pages, simply listen to his music. It’s all there.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11290013464596880706" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_800334681" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/800334681" />
    <a title="Lord Beardsley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/60654-lord-beardsley"><img alt="Lord Beardsley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1181785165p2/60654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/800334681?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lord Beardsley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lord Beardsley" href="/user/show/60654-lord-beardsley">Lord Beardsley</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/60654-lord-beardsley?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer800334681" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5755365562886451227">Oh sheesh, y'all. Not since reading 'Me Talk Pretty One Day' have I laughed as much as I did whilst reading this 457 page opus. <br/><br/>Nonstop LOLS. <br/><br/>This is 100% hardcore, uncut, Morrissey bitching about ingratitude, loathing, meat eaters, and various other things he hates. If you can't handle the HeAT(e) <- ok i tried, then get out of the angry vegetarian kitchen. But if you're like me, and you love bitchery (which autocorrect just changed to "butchery" oh the irony!), walls of text, and unhealthy obs</span>
  <span id="freeText5755365562886451227" style="display:none">Oh sheesh, y'all. Not since reading 'Me Talk Pretty One Day' have I laughed as much as I did whilst reading this 457 page opus. <br /><br />Nonstop LOLS. <br /><br />This is 100% hardcore, uncut, Morrissey bitching about ingratitude, loathing, meat eaters, and various other things he hates. If you can't handle the HeAT(e) &lt;- ok i tried, then get out of the angry vegetarian kitchen. But if you're like me, and you love bitchery (which autocorrect just changed to "butchery" oh the irony!), walls of text, and unhealthy obsessions with dead celebrities…well walk strait this way.<br /><br />Things I didn't like<br /><br />-fat jokes<br />-the overly long account of how he got fucked over in court like Oscar Wilde<br />-being way too concerned with his chart status<br /><br />Really sweet things<br />-saving animals<br />-caring a lot about his fan base<br />-ridiculous moments of random hilarity<br />-when he has to deal with ghost bullshit<br />-when he's a total bitch<br />-how his experience as being from an immigrant family dealing with poverty is reflected in his Mexican and Mexican-American fan base <br />-how much he loves Fresno<br />-how much he loves Mexican gang members with elaborate neck tattoos<br />- that he is a sincere feminist<br />- when he goes on his epic tirade about how much he hates Rough Trade - it's so full of spite that it goes past bitter and into the realms of deep psychedelia <br />- how he refers to his grandma's house as "nannie's lair"<br />- his description of Manchester as basically being a giant grey turd populated by perverted zombie hobos<br />-his really scary ghost story about driving through the Moors - (in a Jesse Pinkman voice) "that shit gave me nightmares, yo."<br /><br />/<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://i.imgur.com/ADIkV.gif">http://i.imgur.com/ADIkV.gif</a><br /><br />his complaining is so, so good<br />how much he hates Geoff Travis from Rough Trade<br />-when he goes on a giant rage-a-thon about how much he hates the fuck out of Rough Trade. It's so ragey that at one point it nearly becomes a delusional psychedelic experience involving Allen Ginsberg leering from atop a wardrobe (just go with me here)<br />- how much he hates Siouxsie Sioux and that one time that he tells her to go the opposite direction to try to catch a cab as she's leaving his flat because she threw too much shade at him and he felt like being a bitch<br />- how much he hates his ex-drummer (who sounds like a total jackoff)<br />- how much he hates the judge who fucked him over (who Michael Stipe referred to as a fuckhead) -how much he loves the hell out of mean old women<br />-the climax of the book when he throws a box of Cheez Its at an audience in Atlanta for eating sandwiches during his live set that he claims “where probably made from rabbit necks and eagle shit”<br />-when he sees Tony Wilson crossing the street and mistakes him for Jerry Springer<br />-when he declares that Tony Wilson never had a good idea in his entire life after he tells him that he should name his next album 'Steven'. <br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.nashvillescene.com/binary/a209/1381950481-god-haters-gonna-hate-eagle.jpg">http://www.nashvillescene.com/binary/...</a><br /><br />-He seems like an incredibly sweet, hopelessly awkward, intelligent, and loving person with absolutely no built-in filter -- and he thinks of himself as ugly and not funny -- which is where he is absolutely wrong. This made me rediscover and fall in love with everything I originally loved so much about his solo music as well as his work with the Smiths and this was a great read that came at just the right time that I needed it, much the same as his music came into my life when I was 13 years old. <br /><br />PROCEED WITH CAUTION - SPOILERS AHEAD - <br /><br />Saying this is a bit ridiculous since Moz himself is happy to spoil just about anything…but still. I like to play fair. <br /><br />I'd like to share my favorite quotes:<br />"The abyss in which I live hasn't the wit to save itself from savage ignorance, and I now feel assured that I am not in the company of my own species (or, at least, I hope I am not, for if I am, then I am they). Dear God, let time pass quickly, and let this end."<br /><br />"In mid-70s Manchester there must be obsessive love of vagina, otherwise your life dooms itself forever."<br /><br />"I am surely a secretive part of some scientific experiment of endurance, or a prank played by God."<br /><br />"'the Smiths' were signed to Rough Trade, Geoff removed his Vivian Stanshall cape and made an impressive effort to erase the old-governess spirit of Rough Trade with a tearless goodbye to their fair trade essence of hiring dwarfs on stilts to pack and shift"<br /><br />"The flat is haunted -- as everyone who calls by testifies (even if the chilled atmosphere is initially assumed to be me). Air of leaden fatigue hangs outside of the bathroom, as if something is standing right there in the hallway. There is also a heavy sense of sadness in the bedroom where I sleep, an atmosphere I am used to leaving behind -- but not finding as I arrive."<br /><br />"My own name is now synonymous with the word 'miserable.'"<br /><br />"I vomit profusely when I discover that the album has been pressed in Japan with Sandie Shaw's version of 'Hand in glove' included. I am so disgusted by this that I beg people to kill me. Many rush forward."<br /><br />"the Smiths as a working unit are assured and agreeable, their main misfortune so far being the way in which they had been sold like a cow at a market to Sire Records. Like Allen Ginsberg perched on the top of your mother's wardrobe, Geoff Travis had looked down smiling his whooping-cough smile as the Smiths lumbered along…"<br /><br />"People are getting a bit fed up with your list of complaints.<br />-'And who do I complain to about THAT?" I ask."<br /><br />"Another review in an American newspaper commented: 'The first 3 tracks on this album are probably the worst 3 songs I have ever heard in my life.' Ah, platitudes."<br /><br />"Geoff leans forward and removes his glasses. 'Do you know why Smiths singles don't go any higher?'<br />I say nothing because the question is horribly rhetorical.<br />'Because they're not good enough.' He puts his glasses back on and shrugs his shoulders. I glance around his office searching for an axe.<br />Some murders are well worth their prison term.”<br /><br />“I storm the gates of Rough Trade in a now familiar maniacal furore. In a state of homicidal seizure I demand to know why the CD image does not repeat the LP image. <br />'But we couldn't fit the entire LP image on the CD because a CD is too small,' says Richard Boon, unhelpfully.<br />'But they managed quite well with Sergeant Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band!” I stomp, suddenly wondering why I continued to bother. I could, instead, be skiing in St Moritz.”<br /><br />“'Er Morrissey, I think there's a grammatical error here – 'who said I lied because I never'...' he aids, helpfully.<br />'Yesssssssssssssssssssssssssss,' I hiss, like an adder on heat, 'it's meant to be there,' and NOW I know how Joan of Arc felt.”<br /><br />“Could things get any worse?<br />Why, yes, little one. Be patient.”<br /><br />“David Bowie, who feeds on the blood of living mammals, rises like Christopher Lee to present a bouquet of flowers to Johnny.”<br /><br />“'The riot ensued when Morrissey instructed the crowd to 'come party', ' says a reliable newshound to the on-the-spot camera, and the very idea of me ever sinking so low as to use the expression 'come party' makes me spray tonight's toddy across the television screen.”<br /><br />“'Morrissey conveys all the worst elements of homosexuality and bestiality,' and I wonder if he could possibly mean me. It is not enough, I note, to represent homosexuality fused with bestiality, but indeed I convey all the very worst elements of both.”<br /><br />“I meet David Bowie for breakfast at a discreet restaurant at the foot of the Hollywood Hills. Both standing at the buffet with our empty plates, David hovers over what are horrifically called 'cold cuts'. I nestle up beside him.<br />'David, you're not actually going to eat that stuff, are you?'<br />Rumbled, he snaps: 'Oh, you must be HELL to live with.'<br />'Yes, I am,' I say proudly, as David changes course and sidles off towards the fruit salad, and another soul is saved from the burning fires of self-imposed eternal damnation.<br />David quietly tells me, 'You know, I've had so much sex and drugs that I can't believe I'm still alive,' and I loudly tell him, 'You know, I've had SO LITTLE sex and drugs that I can't believe I'm still alive.”<br /><br />“It is a simple truth that everything in life ends badly – few people die in a fit of hysterics.”<br /><br />“She is very much as I had expected – a physical blancmange that is six parts Kate O'Mara, two parts Myra Hindley and two parts Fenella Fielding.”<br /><br />“EMI unsurprisingly become bored with the battle of the sea goats.”<br /><br />“and for this I scrub Gill's name off my 'In Sympathy At the Loss of Your Pet Goat' list.”<br /><br />“'Why did you mention Battersea in that song?' is his opening gambit.<br />'Because it rhymed with Fatty,' I reply”<br /><br />“In another bar, a screech-owl female frump begins a beer sodden tire-slashing attack on Chrissie with, 'You used to mean SO MUCH to me,' to which Chrissie breaks in with, 'Yes. But I don't now – so fuck off.'”<br /><br />“I stay home and put the kettle on, talking aloud to myself and pondering on how even Billie Holiday had sex. 'Oh that's so Morrissey – he doesn't even turn up for his own album launch,' says someone with scurvy and rickets.”<br /><br />“'I suppose you're enormous in Cleveland?' asks David Bowie. <br />'No,' I reply, utterly baffled.<br />'Oh.' He slumps.”<br /><br />“Back in New York, David Bowie asks me, 'Do you, er, still have the same band?'<br />'Yes,' I say, and he looks downwards.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="5755365562886451227" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6059320073298423070">I was going to write a grand, illuminating review of Mozzer's book, but alas I just cannot gather my thoughts cohesively enough to do justice. Therefore, these are my disjointed thoughts and quotes that spoke to me:<br/><br/>~This book is a treasure trove of music and film references. I found myself making playlists from the get-go. It's fun to hear and see what has inspired and continues to inspire Morrissey.<br/><br/>~From the very first page this was one of the most brilliantly written books I've ever read. It s</span>
  <span id="freeText6059320073298423070" style="display:none">I was going to write a grand, illuminating review of Mozzer's book, but alas I just cannot gather my thoughts cohesively enough to do justice. Therefore, these are my disjointed thoughts and quotes that spoke to me:<br /><br />~This book is a treasure trove of music and film references. I found myself making playlists from the get-go. It's fun to hear and see what has inspired and continues to inspire Morrissey.<br /><br />~From the very first page this was one of the most brilliantly written books I've ever read. It sounds kind of dumb, but this is the only memoir he could have written; it just seems so completely from his core. By turns he's insightful, poetic, horrified, witty, baffled, sincere, hurt, enraged and weary. I could hear him in my head with every written word.<br /><br />~pg. 96: <i>I ask myself if there is an irresponsible aspect in relaying thoughts of pain as inspiration, and I wonder whether Housman actually infected the sensitives further, and pulled them back into additional darkness. </i><br /><br />~I love how he referred to his lyrics throughout the book.<br /><br />~p.g 412 <i>Nowhere are there more natural smiles than those of a welcoming audience.</i><br /><br />~I'm proud of his remaining Morrissey, which is an absolute absurd thing to say, but he's always stayed true to himself. To know yourself, to be yourself at all times, no matter the expense, what an incredibly difficult and brave thing to do in the world we live in.<br /><br />~pg. 439 <i>Finally aware of ourselves as forever being in opposition, the solution to all predicaments is the goodness of privacy in a warm room with books.</i><br /><br />~Haters don't even bother, this autobiography is perfection.<br /><br />~pg. 140 <i>I am singing the truth by myself, which might also be the truth of others...and give me a whole life...let the voice speak up for once and for all...</i></span>

<div id="review_800167731" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/800167731" />
    <a title="Davidg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14399908-davidg"><img alt="Davidg" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/800167731?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Davidg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Davidg" href="/user/show/14399908-davidg">Davidg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer800167731" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10764828627746884507">Imagine the story of The Smiths written as a bad novel narrated by a whining teenager - a sub sub sub 'Catcher in the Rye'. And here it is.<br/><br/>It takes several pages for the idea of paragraphs to take hold but even then they can start talking of one thing and end up being about something else. No wonder he has no idea of splitting the books into chapters.<br/><br/>Everyone who doesn't fall into raptures at the sight of our whinging hero is immediately branded as some hopeless loser. So, every teacher, EVERY s</span>
  <span id="freeText10764828627746884507" style="display:none">Imagine the story of The Smiths written as a bad novel narrated by a whining teenager - a sub sub sub 'Catcher in the Rye'. And here it is.<br /><br />It takes several pages for the idea of paragraphs to take hold but even then they can start talking of one thing and end up being about something else. No wonder he has no idea of splitting the books into chapters.<br /><br />Everyone who doesn't fall into raptures at the sight of our whinging hero is immediately branded as some hopeless loser. So, every teacher, EVERY single teacher is a lonely miserable frustrated old bag or bachelor. Really? I grew up just a few miles away and we had young teachers, trendy teachers, single teachers, married teachers, but that wouldn't fit our heroes view of the world. When an interviewer ends an interview at the set time, as dictated by TV, his ' clothe look better without him in them'. <br /><br />Of course, the rules that should apply to the rest of the world don't apply to our hero. The dreadful Music Business put contracts in front of him, written just to confuse him. They rip him off left, right and centre. But when his band mates sign the same piece of paper, he insists that because they signed it, they should be held to it. It is ok for him to rip others off, even though they are his friends. All my Smiths albums list the FOUR members of the band and performing fees are always split between band members. But that seems unfair to our hero, so he takes 60% of his colleagues earnings and thinks they should be grateful to receive their 10% whilst he takes 40%. And then he'll take the songwriting income on top of that. Not surprisingly he loses the court case. But that's because the establishment hates northern working class geniuses. And all judges apparently own huge mansions and go hunting all the time - really?<br /><br />There is no insight, no reflection, no change. Our hero is fully formed from the moment of birth. Apparently there was no moment when he became a vegetarian, no time when he learned something. That might mean he wasn't always perfect. And don't expect to be told about writing songs or what they might reveal. There will be no self revelation. Just another long extended moan. Just like this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10764828627746884507" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1462009940" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1462009940" />
    <a title="Carol" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/49786241-carol"><img alt="Carol" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459792100p2/49786241.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1462009940?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol" href="/user/show/49786241-carol">Carol</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1462009940" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6848450847156469417">I listened to this audiobook in small doses, which may have even toned down Morrissey's anger. Moz is one angry dude. I have no doubt that he was wronged by the government of Great Britain, the press, and fellow musicians and targeted for his social and personal beliefs. Still, the obsessive catalog of misdeeds against him made for an uncomfortable listen as his absolute inability to let go of seemingly every wrong was disturbing. <br/>As a champion of misfits, it was depressing to hear multiple inst</span>
  <span id="freeText6848450847156469417" style="display:none">I listened to this audiobook in small doses, which may have even toned down Morrissey's anger. Moz is one angry dude. I have no doubt that he was wronged by the government of Great Britain, the press, and fellow musicians and targeted for his social and personal beliefs. Still, the obsessive catalog of misdeeds against him made for an uncomfortable listen as his absolute inability to let go of seemingly every wrong was disturbing. <br />As a champion of misfits, it was depressing to hear multiple instances of him mocking overweight people. He described a chubby girl knocking on his car window as a "terrible" moment. Really?<br />Remembering his glee in describing Julia Burchill's physique and realizing he preys on people's vulnerability shows he is hardly a spokesman for outsiders. <br />Just when you think he is leading up to saying something decent about someone, he finishes with an insult-i.e. comparing Michael Stipe's voice to John Denver's? <br /> <br />Two and 1/2 stars because it is Morrissey and he certainly cracked himself open, though likely more for himself than the reader.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6848450847156469417" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16800589615791366828" style="display:none"><br />I probably would've given this five stars had it not been for the absurdly long section on the Smiths trial. <div id="review_754204181" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/754204181" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6967858-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599732368p2/6967858.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/754204181?book_show_action=true">Nov 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/6967858-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer754204181" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6212951794747901506">As a long time Smiths/Morrissey fan I was excited to get my hands on this book, and largely it doesn’t disappoint. I would go so far as to say that this is one of the most incredible books I have ever read. It’s not your typical autobiography, so much so that for the first page or so it completely threw me. The account of his early years and the rise of The Smiths are full of sadness, wit, tenderness and betrayal. His description of his school days, and the vile bullying teachers of St Mary’s, i</span>
  <span id="freeText6212951794747901506" style="display:none">As a long time Smiths/Morrissey fan I was excited to get my hands on this book, and largely it doesn’t disappoint. I would go so far as to say that this is one of the most incredible books I have ever read. It’s not your typical autobiography, so much so that for the first page or so it completely threw me. The account of his early years and the rise of The Smiths are full of sadness, wit, tenderness and betrayal. His description of his school days, and the vile bullying teachers of St Mary’s, is particularly effecting; almost like something written by Mervyn Peake. He obviously had some scores to settle and you get the impression that years of silence have finally found an outlet. There are some targets you wouldn’t expect, whilst others who have since done him wrong are discussed with tenderness. It does occasionally descend into bitchiness, and there are a few roll your eyes moments as Morrissey storms out of restaurants because someone at his table has had the audacity to order meat. Yet he seems fully aware of his own shortcomings. What really comes through in this book, especially the first third, is his compassion for those around him; and his hurt and bewilderment at the various betrayals (either real or perceived it is difficult to say). There’s a lot of humour too, some of it tongue in cheek, which I think a lot of people will miss. There’s even a mini ghost story which takes place on Saddleworth Moor which is so well described that it could keep you awake at night like it did me. <br /><br />Unlike other reviewers I actually found the section dedicated to the court case fascinating as Morrissey builds a very strong case for his own persecution at the hands of the establishment. I felt that this was an important point, and I don’t blame Morrissey for dwelling on it. He basically laughs at the idea of free speech existing in England. This is a man who’d previously taken pot shots at royalty and Margaret Thatcher, so it’s easy to believe that they wanted to bring him down. And why did the case focus on Morrissey rather than the Morrissey &amp; Marr partnership? <br /><br />I got the impression Morrissey wrote this over many years as the front and end sections feel almost as if they were written by different people. For me the end section felt flat, and slightly smug, where Morrissey details his comeback starting with You Are the Quarry. This was where the book started to lose its grip on me. You can see how he wanted to bring the story full circle and show how all his trials paid off in the end. At one point he describes how, on stage in Manchester, he can see the building where he once worked in the basement as an Inland Revenue clerk which rounds off his story quite well. The endless details of shows and cities and hotels, though, felt a little empty as if it were only required to fill up the word count.<br /><br />Ultimately, though this is the story of Morrissey, it is so packed with wisdom and insight that it is lifted above mere autobiography and becomes something akin to The Bell Jar. Anyone merely looking for revelations will miss what makes this a great book. It is the story of a misfit boy who wanted to sing, went through various trials along the way, fought and fought and fought and fought to follow his dream, did it all his way, and came out a winner in the end.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6212951794747901506" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_834155538" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/834155538" />
    <a title="Brian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/826373-brian"><img alt="Brian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442508276p2/826373.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/834155538?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brian" href="/user/show/826373-brian">Brian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer834155538" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13619103680899406545">Morrissey's long-ish autobiography is at its best when he is writing about the things he is most enthusiastic about (his teenage love of the New York Dolls, David Bowie, T. Rex, Jobraith, the Sex Pistols, and the Ramones; his best friendships; his fans; the musical output of The Smiths). When he is a critic (his bleak upbringing in Manchester; the litigious entanglements of The [Post-] Smiths; NME), he becomes insufferable (mostly because he is long-winded in these sections), though his criticis</span>
  <span id="freeText13619103680899406545" style="display:none">Morrissey's long-ish autobiography is at its best when he is writing about the things he is most enthusiastic about (his teenage love of the New York Dolls, David Bowie, T. Rex, Jobraith, the Sex Pistols, and the Ramones; his best friendships; his fans; the musical output of The Smiths). When he is a critic (his bleak upbringing in Manchester; the litigious entanglements of The [Post-] Smiths; NME), he becomes insufferable (mostly because he is long-winded in these sections), though his criticisms aren't totally unwarranted. Keep in mind, this is the man who once sang, "I was looking for a job / and I found a job / And heaven knows I'm miserable now." His prose is a little stuffy at times, but that is, in the end, refreshing when compared with the prose style of most rock autobiographies. I am not a huge Smiths/Morrissey fan and think that if I were, this book would have resonated with me more than it did. That being said, any fan of rock music can gather something about the artistic process and the downright unethical business machinations of the music industry by reading this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13619103680899406545" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1133697836">
  <td colspan="2">

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('4LkvfYdOKgxmR0cfD07oq76sCmYuAC1IDeS0SnZ8rSKvEvbq3fpyWk6fAlsopT0qHuq7HjxdVw/iA2sOE2qXXw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('E52J9mIj5mCt2w2nzYIV46xMVtj9YI2kg3lkkLgzyGVcNlBhOJe+NoUDSOPqacBiDArnoO899+NsnrvU3SXyGA==')})
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_29099287'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29099287-set-the-boy-free?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Set the Boy Free<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/1016400.Johnny_Marr?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Johnny Marr<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.18 avg rating &mdash; 2,667 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2016\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer13180153414988215338\">The long-awaited memoir from the legendary guitarist and cofounder of the seminal British band The Smiths.\n\nAn artist who helped define a period in popular culture, Johnny Marr tells his story in a memoir as vivid and arresting as his music. The Smit<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13180153414988215338\" style=\"display:none\">The long-awaited memoir from the legendary guitarist and cofounder of the seminal British band The Smiths.\n\nAn artist who helped define a period in popular culture, Johnny Marr tells his story in a memoir as vivid and arresting as his music. The Smiths, the band with the signature sound he cofounded, remains one of the most beloved bands ever, and have a profound influence on a number of acts that followed—from the Stone Roses, Suede, Blur, and Radiohead to Oasis, The Libertines, and Arctic Monkeys.\n\nMarr recalls his childhood growing up in the northern working-class city of Manchester, in a house filled with music. He takes us back to the summer of 1982 when, at eighteen, he sought out one Stephen Morrissey to form a new band they called The Smiths. Marr invites fans on stage, on the road, and in the studio for the five years The Smiths were together and how after a rapid ascent, the working-class teenage rock star enjoyed and battled with the perks of success until ideological differences, combined with his much publicized strained relationships with fellow band mates, caused him to leave in 1987. Marr’s “escape” as he calls it, ensured the beginning of the end for one of the most influential groups of a generation. But The Smiths’ end was only the beginning for Marr. The bona-fide guitar hero continues to experiment and evolve in his solo career to this day, playing with Paul McCartney, Pretenders, Modest Mouse, Oasis and collaborating today’s most creative and renowned artists. \n\nRising above and beyond the personal struggles and bitter feuds, Marr delivers the story of his music and his band, sharing the real insights of a man who has made music his life, and finally giving fans what they’ve truly been waiting for.\n\n <\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13180153414988215338\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return Godfathers of alternative rock, they reinvented music in the post-punk era, creating a new sound—dark, hypnotic, and intense—that would influence U2, Morrissey, R.E.M., Radiohead, and numerous others. The story is now legendary: in 1980, on the heels of their groundbreaking debut, Unknown Pleasures, and on the eve of their first U.S. tour, the band was rent asunder by the tragic death of their enigmatic lead singer, Ian Curtis. Yet in the mere three years they were together, Joy Division produced two landmark albums and a handful of singles—including the iconic anthem &quot;Love Will Tear Us Apart&quot;—that continue to have a powerful resonance.\n\nNow, for the first time, their story is told by one of their own. In Unknown Pleasures, founding member and bass player Peter Hook recounts how four young men from Manchester and Salisbury, with makeshift instruments and a broken-down van, rose from the punk scene to create a haunting, atmospheric music that would define a generation. Peter talks with eye-opening candor and reflection about the suicide of Ian Curtis; the band\'s friendships and fallouts; the evolution of their sound and image; and the larger-than-life characters who formed a vital part of the Joy Division legend, including Factory Records founder Tony Wilson and producer Martin Hannett. Furthermore, The Beatles have opened their personal and management archives specifically for this project, allowing the unprecedented release of photographs which they took along their ride to fame, as well as fascinating documents and memorabilia from their homes and offices. What a book The Beatles Anthology is! Each page is brimming with personal stories and rare vintage images. Snapshots from their family collections take us back to the days when John Lennon, Paul McCartney, George Harrison and Richard Starkey were just boys growing up in Liverpool. They talk in turn about those early years and how they came to join the band that would make them known around the world as John, Paul, George and Ringo. Then, weaving back and forth, they tell the astonishing story of life as The Beatles: the first rough gigs, the phenomenon of their rise to fame, the musical and social change of their heyday, all the way through to their breakup. From the time Ringo tried to take this drum kit home on the bus to their much anticipated audience with Elvis, from the making of the Sgt. Pepper album to their last photo session together at John\'s house, The Beatles Anthology is a once-in-a-lifetime collection of The Beatles\' own memories.\n\nInterwoven with these are the recollections of such associates as road manager Neil Aspinall, producer George Martin and spokesman Derek Taylor. And included in the vast array of photographs are materials from both Apple and EMI, who also opened their archives for this project. This, indeed, is the inside story, providing a wealth of previously unpublished material in both word and image.\n\nCreated with their full cooperation, The Beatles Anthology is, in effect, The Beatles\' autobiography. Like their music has been a part of so many of our lives, it\'s warm, frank, funny, poignant and bold.           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5661015409194599254">Steven Patrick Morrissey (born 22 May 1959), known primarily as Morrissey, is an English lyricist and singer. He rose to prominence in the 1980s as the lyricist and vocalist of the alternative rock band the Smiths. The band was highly successful in the UK but broke up in 1987, and Morrissey began a solo career, making the top ten of the UK Singles Chart in the United Kingdom on ten occasions. Wide</span>
  <span id="freeText5661015409194599254" style="display:none">Steven Patrick Morrissey (born 22 May 1959), known primarily as Morrissey, is an English lyricist and singer. He rose to prominence in the 1980s as the lyricist and vocalist of the alternative rock band the Smiths. The band was highly successful in the UK but broke up in 1987, and Morrissey began a solo career, making the top ten of the UK Singles Chart in the United Kingdom on ten occasions. Widely regarded as an important innovator in indie music, Morrissey has been described by music magazine NME as "one of the most influential artists ever," and The Independent has stated "most pop stars have to be dead before they reach the iconic status he has reached in his lifetime." Pitchfork Media has called him "one of the most singular figures in Western popular culture from the last 20 years."<br /><br />Morrissey's lyrics have been described as "dramatic, bleak, funny vignettes about doomed relationships, lonely nightclubs, the burden of the past and the prison of the home." He is also noted for his unique baritone vocal style, though he's known to use falsetto for emphasis. His forthright, often contrarian opinions, especially on the subject of race, have led to a number of media controversies, and he has also attracted media attention for his advocacy of vegetarianism and animal rights. He has also been noted for his quiff haircut as well as his performance style of his early years.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5661015409194599254" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
